•  Gene expression profiling
•  Medical Genomics
•  Melanoma
•  Skin cancer
•  Transcriptomics
•  Tumour Biomarkers
•  Gene Expression Analysis in Cancer Genomics
Introduction

Melanoma, originating from melanocytes in the skin’s outer layer, is an
aggressive form of cancer. It is known for its rapid spread and resistance
to therapy, leading to a high fatality rate [1]. While the five-year survival
rate for early-stage melanoma is nearly 100%, this figure significantly
decreases to just 20% for cases with distant metastasis [2]. Surgery is
the mainstay of treatment for early-stage melanoma; however, many
patients are not detected until advanced disease, at which point the
prognosis deteriorates significantly. Currently, targeted therapies are
only recommended for patients with BRAF-mutant melanoma, and there
is a lack of sufficient therapeutic evidence for other types of mutations
[3]. Therefore, in-depth study of the mechanisms of melanoma
development, identification of key biomarkers, and exploration of key
target genes to improve the diagnostic value of melanoma is essential for
the development of new therapeutic strategies and improvement of
patient prognosis.
With the rapid development of therapeutic technologies, the medical
community has continued to explore and experiment with innovative
approaches in the field of melanoma treatment. Although
immunotherapy, a breakthrough in melanoma treatment, has shown
significant efficacy in some patients, treatment strategies for advanced
melanoma, especially in patients who have progressed after first-line
treatment, still face many challenges [4]. The failure of many patients to
derive the expected clinical benefit from immunotherapy may be related
to the remarkable heterogeneity and complex molecular subtypes of
melanoma. The heterogeneity of melanoma, which results in significant
differences in disease progression and treatment response in patients,

arises from the genetic and phenotypic diversity of tumor cells and their
interactions with the tumor microenvironment (TME). Cells in the TME,
such as immune, stromal, and vascular cells, interact with tumor cells
and collectively determine the likelihood of disease progression,
therapeutic efficacy, and therapeutic resistance [5]. However, previous
studies have mainly focused on tumor cells, neglecting the critical role of
TME in melanoma development. Single-cell RNA sequencing (scRNA-
seq) is a powerful tool for elucidating the complex biological mechanisms
and gene expression patterns within melanoma cells. By examining the
variability in gene expression across individual cells, this technique
captures the randomness and dynamism inherent in the transcriptional
profiles of key genes. With its high-resolution capability, scRNA-seq
uncovers the full spectrum of cellular heterogeneity, offering detailed
insights into the molecular landscape of skin cutaneous melanoma
(SKCM) and advancing our comprehensive knowledge of the disease [6].
In this study, we comprehensively assessed the molecular subtypes of
melanoma, the characteristics of the tumor microenvironment, and their
impact on patient prognosis using multi-omics analysis and machine
learning techniques. By analyzing the TCGA-SKCM database and the
GEO cohort, we identified three melanoma molecular subtypes with
significantly different prognostic features. This finding not only reveals
the diversity and complexity of melanoma molecular subtypes but also
provides new targets for personalized treatment. Additionally, we
constructed a machine learning-driven prognostic signature (MLDS) that
significantly improved the accuracy of prognostic prediction for
melanoma patients. Compared to traditional prognostic models, MLDS

integrates multiple machine learning algorithms to more accurately
identify high-risk patients, offering robust support for clinical decision-
making. Our prognostic signature further identified a new prognostic
gene, AGPAT2. Through single-cell RNA sequencing, we meticulously
characterized the oncogenic role of AGPAT2 and its associated signaling
pathways in the TME of melanoma. This finding offers a new perspective
for the study of melanoma molecular mechanisms. We confirmed the
importance of AGPAT2 for melanoma proliferation, migration, and poor
prognosis by knocking down AGPAT2 with small interfering RNA in both
in vitro and in vivo experiments. These results not only validate the
prognostic value of AGPAT2 but also provide potential therapeutic
targets for future treatment strategies. Moreover, this study highlights the
critical role of the TME in disease progression. By analyzing TME
characteristics, we provide new insights and tools for personalized
treatment and prognostic assessment of melanoma. In summary, our
study has made significant progress in the research of melanoma
molecular subtypes and prognostic biomarkers, and it offers a new
direction for future clinical applications and the development of treatment
strategies.
Materials and methods
Sources of raw data
The flowchart of this study is shown in Fig. 1. We retrieved multi-omics
data for the TCGA-SKCM project from the UCSC Xena platform (https://
xenabrowser.net/), including mRNA expression profiles, long non-coding

RNA (lncRNA) expression profiles, miRNA expression profiles,
methylation microarray data, gene mutation data, and associated clinical
information. To ensure data consistency and comparability, all
expression data were first converted into TPM (Transcripts Per Million)
format to eliminate the effects of sequencing depth and gene length,
followed by log2 transformation to reduce data skewness and meet the
assumptions required for subsequent statistical analyses. Additionally,
we downloaded three independent external validation datasets
(GSE19234, GSE59455, and GSE65904) from the Gene Expression
Omnibus (GEO) database to verify the reliability and generalizability of
our findings [7,8,9]. To eliminate systematic biases that may arise from
different platforms and experimental batches, we used the ComBat
function provided by the “sva” package in R for batch effect correction
and further enhanced data consistency using the removeBatchEffect
function from the “limma” package [10]. During data preprocessing, we
also conducted rigorous quality control, including filtering out low-
expressed genes (e.g., TPM < 1) and checking sample grouping
consistency through principal component analysis (PCA) and hierarchical
clustering. Finally, all standardized and corrected data were saved as
normalized matrices for subsequent analyses, including differential
expression analysis, survival analysis, and functional enrichment
analysis.
Fig. 1
[image]
The research approach of the entire paper

Full size image
Single-cell sequencing data download and processing
Single-cell sequencing data from three cases of melanoma were
retrieved from the dataset GSE215120, as referenced in the study [11].
The Read10X_h5 function was employed to process the h5 file format
data, as detailed in another study [12]. Utilizing the “Seurat” R package,
Seurat objects were created and subjected to a stringent filtration
process. This involved selecting cells that expressed a gene count
between 200 and 7000, had less than 20% mitochondrial genes, and
contained less than 10% hemoglobin genes, following the guidelines
outlined in the literature [12]. To mitigate the impact of batch effects
among the three samples, the “harmony” R package was implemented.
The single-cell sequencing data underwent a series of standardization
processes, including normalization via the “NormalizeData” function. This
was complemented by scaling, batch effect correction, and
dimensionality reduction, focusing on highly variable genes. Once the
cells were categorized into distinct clusters, the cell types were identified
and annotated based on the specific gene expression profiles
characteristic of each subgroup. The developmental trajectory analysis
was then conducted using the Monocle2 algorithm, which was applied to
the inferred tumor cells. The analysis utilized default parameters
following the downsampling and unit sorting of the data, as per the
methodology described in the relevant publication [13]. Furthermore, the
“Cellcall” R package was employed to deduce the intercellular
communication networks and internal regulatory signals. This package

compiles KEGG pathway-based ligand-receptor-transcription factor (L-R-
TF) axis datasets. It facilitates the inference of cell-to-cell communication
by integrating prior knowledge of L-R-TF interactions with the expression
levels of ligands/receptors and the activity of downstream transcription
factors, as reported in the study [14].
Multi-omics consensus cluster analysis
In our research, we utilized the “getElites” function from the “MOVICS” R
package to identify key genes. For continuous data types, including
mRNA, lncRNA, miRNA, and methylation profiles, we applied the “mad”
method to filter and select the top 1500 most variable genes. We then
switched to the “Cox” method to integrate clinical data, focusing on
genes with significant prognostic value at a p-value cutoff of less than
0.05. For mutation data, treated as a binary variable, we first used the
“oncoPrint” function from the maftools package to identify the top 5000
genes with the highest mutation rates. We refined this list by prioritizing
the top 1% of genes with the highest mutation frequency using the “freq”
method. After consolidating the findings from these five dimensions, we
moved on to determine the optimal number of clusters for our study
using the “getClustNum” function from “MOVICS.” This function uses the
Clustering Predictive Index (CPI), gap statistics, and profile coefficients
to estimate the number of subgroups, leading us to classify the samples
into three distinct subtypes. We then conducted cluster analysis with the
“getMOIC” function, applying 10 clustering algorithms: CIMLR,
ConsensusClustering, SNF, iClusterBayes, PINSPlus, moCluster,
NEMO, IntNMF, COCA, and LRA. The “methodslist” parameter in the

“getMOIC” function was set with these algorithms, following the default
parameters of the MOVICS package, to produce clustering results for
each. Subsequently, we used the “getConsensusMOIC” function to
merge the clustering outcomes from the different algorithms, employing
consensus clustering to strengthen the robustness of the clustering by
integrating the results into a single classification. To delve deeper into
the regulatory mechanisms, we constructed transcriptional regulatory
networks, or regulons, using the “RTN” R package. We also assessed
the distribution of immune checkpoints across the identified subtypes,
estimating the immune and stromal scores of tumor tissues with the
“estimate” R package. For subtype stability evaluation, we began by
validating the clustering results against subtype-specific biomarkers in a
validation cohort. We further compared the consensus clustering with the
NTP and PAM classifiers for consistency, following the methodologies
outlined in the referenced study [15]. This comprehensive approach
ensures a thorough evaluation of the subtypes and their stability,
contributing to a deeper understanding of the underlying biology and
potential therapeutic implications.
Integrating machine learning algorithms to build
predictive models
We conducted a comprehensive evaluation of the prognostic significance
of 300 mRNAs related to molecular subtyping in both the TCGA and
GEO cohorts. This assessment was performed through univariate Cox
regression analyses, providing a foundational understanding of the
association between each mRNA and patient outcomes. Building on

these results, we ventured into the realm of machine learning to develop
predictive models. We employed 101 combinations of 10 distinct
machine-learning algorithms. Each algorithm underwent a rigorous 10-
fold cross-validation process, with the TCGA cohort used for training and
the GSE19234, GSE22153, and GSE65904 cohorts used for validation.
The algorithms we utilized included a mix of established and advanced
methods, such as StepCox, Lasso, Ridge, partial least squares Cox
regression (plsRcox), Cox Boost, Random Survival Forest (RSF),
Gradient Boosting Machine (GBM), Elastic Net (Enet), Supervised
Principal Component Analysis (SuperPC), and Survival Support Vector
Machine (Survival-SVM).
Our machine learning approach is similar to the combination of 10
machine learning algorithms used in the study by Liu et al. [16, 17]. We
used the same algorithms: StepCox, Lasso, Ridge, plsRcox, CoxBoost,
RSF, GBM, Enet, SuperPC, and Survival-SVM. Both studies employed
101 algorithm combinations and used the concordance index (C-index)
as the metric for evaluating model performance. The primary difference
lies in the cross-validation method: we used 10-fold cross-validation,
while Liu et al. used leave-one-out cross-validation. Our main objective
was to identify the most valuable prognostic and immunotherapeutic
features, as indicated by the highest C-index. The C-index served as the
benchmark for assessing the predictive accuracy of our models. After a
meticulous comparison of the C-index across all models tested in the
validation set, the model that achieved the highest rank was identified as
the optimal machine learning-driven signature (MLDS).

Construction of the nomogram
Participants were categorized into high and low MLDS score groups
based on the median MLDS score. To evaluate the prognostic disparity
between these groups, we deployed the “survival” and “survminer” R
packages. Furthermore, the “timeROC” R package was employed to
determine the AUC values. A column chart was constructed to estimate
the one-, three-, and five-year overall survival (OS) probabilities, with risk
score, age, and clinical stage serving as independent prognostic
indicators. The precision of the column-line graph was appraised through
the generation of ROC curves. We conducted consistency index analysis
and decision curve analysis (DCA) to evaluate the incremental value of
the column-line diagram in conjunction with clinical characteristics.
Lastly, mutation data from the TCGA cohort were extracted and
normalized into a binary matrix. We calculated the tumor mutation
burden (TMB) and mutation frequency for the MLDS scoring group to
further elucidate the genetic landscape associated with MLDS.
Immune cell infiltration landscape and tumor
microenvironment score
Based on gene expression data from the Cancer Genome Atlas, we
used the R package “estimate” to infer the presence and number of
mesenchymal stromal and immune cells in tumor tissues and to measure
tumor purity [18]. To delve deeper into the immune cell composition of
the SKCM tumor microenvironment (TME), we accessed the TIMER 2.0
database, a comprehensive resource providing multiple methods of

immune cell analysis and evaluation. Single-sample genomic enrichment
analysis (ssGSEA) was then applied to measure the relative prevalence
of various immune cell populations in the samples and their associated
functions. This approach provides us with a nuanced understanding of
the immune environment within the TME and helps to explore the
interactions between tumor cells and their surrounding immune cells in
greater detail.
Immunotherapy, chemotherapy, and enrichment analysis
Xu et al. have developed an online resource that compiles a repository of
genes implicated in cancer and immunological processes, which serves
as a valuable reference in our research [19]. Additionally, Mariathasan’s
research has identified a set of genes that are responsive to anti-PD-L1
therapies. To explore the potential link between these genetic profiles
and the risk scores, we quantified the expression of these gene sets
using the Gene Set Variation Analysis (GSVA) method and depicted the
results with the aid of the “ggcor” R package [20]. The Cancer
Immunome Atlas (TCIA) web tool provides comprehensive
immunogenomic analysis results (https://tcia.at/home). The Immune
Profile Score (IPS) is a tumor immunogenicity score ranging from 0 to 10
that is used to predict effective responses to ICIs [21]. In addition, we
determined the semi-inhibitory concentration (IC50) values for a range of
chemotherapeutic agents commonly used in the treatment of melanoma
using the “pRRophetic” R software package. This analysis helps to
understand the sensitivity of melanoma cells to various treatments [22].
To gain insight into the biological pathways and functions associated with

the different MLDS scoring groups, we performed a single genome
enrichment analysis (GSEA). With this approach, we were able to
identify changes in gene ontology (GO) and KEGG signaling pathway
activation to gain more insight into the molecular mechanisms behind
MLDS scoring.
Functional experiments of melanoma cells
Cell culture and siRNA transfection methods for A875 and A375 cell lines
have been described in our previous study [23]. The sequence of siRNA
used for AGPAT2 is shown: 5’ CCUGUGUGUCAUCGUCUCCAA. After
successful transfection of siRNA, we verified by Cell counting kit-8,
colony formation, Wound-healing assay, and transwell assay that the
function of AGPAT2 in melanoma such as proliferation migration was
used in a previous bioinformatics study [24].
Subcutaneous tumor xenografts and
Immunofluorescence in nude mice
Healthy four-week-old male nude mice were purchased from Zhejiang
Life River Laboratory and housed under specific pathogen-free
conditions. In this study, all animal care and handling procedures were in
accordance with the National Institutes of Health Guide for the Care and
Use of Laboratory Animals and approved by the Animal Care and Use
Committee of the First Hospital of Wenzhou Medical University. Nude
mice were euthanised at the end of the experiment. The choice of
euthanasia method was based on ensuring that the animals were rapidly

rendered unconscious in a painless and fearless state and minimising
pain and anxiety during the death process. For mice, we used an
inhalational anaesthetic euthanasia method. Specifically, mice were
anaesthetised by inhalation of isoflurane or sevoflurane prior to
euthanasia to ensure that they rapidly lost consciousness. Subsequently,
euthanasia is carried out by intravenous injection of an overdose of
sodium pentobarbital (100 mg/kg), a drug that rapidly causes cardiac and
respiratory arrest and ultimately loss of brain function. This method is
considered a humane and effective euthanasia because it ensures that
the animal dies painlessly and unconsciously. During euthanasia, the
animal is placed in a quiet, darkened environment to minimise external
stimuli. Euthanasia is performed by professionally trained technicians to
ensure the accuracy and humanity of the operation. The A375 control
and Si-AGPAT2 cell lines were cultured to the logarithmic growth phase,
rinsed twice with PBS, and cells were collected. The cell concentration
was adjusted to 1 × 107 cells/ml. 100 µl of cell suspension was injected
subcutaneously into the right abdomen of each 6-week-old female nude
mouse (BALB/c-nu), three per group. The length and width of the
tumours were measured with calipers every three days and the body
weights were recorded. Tumour volume was calculated as Volume
(mm³) = 0.5 × length (mm) × width (mm)². On the 21st day after injection,
mice were executed, and tumors were excised and weighed.
Immunofluorescence (IF) staining analysis for HMB45 (affinity, Jiangsu,
China), and Ki-67 (affinity, Jiangsu, China) was performed according to
the manufacturer’s instructions. Images were acquired and positive
expression levels of the above markers were measured using ImageJ

software.
Statistical analysis
Our statistical analyses were conducted using R software, version 4.2.1.
To evaluate the prognostic significance and compare survival curves
among different patient subgroups within our datasets, we employed
Kaplan-Meier survival analysis coupled with the log-rank test. When
assessing the association between continuous variables across two
distinct groups, we relied on the nonparametric Wilcoxon rank sum test
and applied the Bonferroni correction to control the false-positive rate.
Additionally, we examined the strength and direction of relationships
between variables through Spearman’s correlation analysis. In our
analyses, a threshold of P < 0.05 was set as the benchmark for statistical
significance.
Results
Multi-omics to identify SKCM prognostically relevant
molecular subtypes
In this study, TCGA-SKCM was used for molecular subtyping by multi-
omics, and the GSE19234, GSE59455, and GSE65904 cohorts were
used as validation cohorts for subtyping. After effective preprocessing for
all data quality, the principal component analysis showed the three GEO
cohorts after the removal of batch effects (Fig. 2A). We then
independently identified three molecular subtypes of melanoma patients,

cancer subtypes (CSs), from 10 multi-omics integrated clustering
algorithms, and determined the classification of the subtypes by a
combination of cluster prediction indices, gap statistical analysis, and
silhouette score characteristics (Fig. 2B, C ). Subsequently, we further
integrated the clustering results with the molecular expression patterns of
transcriptomics, epigenetics, and somatic mutations to explore the
clustering process and molecular characteristics of the three subtypes
(Fig. 2D-F). Kaplan-Meier curves showed that subtype 2 (CS2) exhibited
the worst overall survival (OS) among all the subtypes evaluated, while
CS1 showed the best prognostic characteristics (Fig. 2G).
Fig. 2
[image]
Molecular subtyping of SKCM based on multi-omics consensus analysis.
(A) Distribution of each dataset after batch correction by PCA algorithm.
(B) Cluster prediction index and gap statistical analysis for multi-omics
clustering. (C) Sample similarity across subgroups was assessed by
calculating Silhouette scores. (D) Consensus heatmap of integrated
subtypes based on marker genes, including mRNAs, lncRNAs, miRNAs,
DNA CpG methylation sites, and mutated genes. (E) Clustering of SKCM
patients using 10 state-of-the-art multi-omics methods. (F) Consensus
clustering matrix for the three prognostic subtypes based on these 10
algorithms. (G) Survival curves of patients with the three molecular
subtypes
Full size image

Classification of molecular subtypes of the integrative
consensus and characterization of subtype immunity
Continuing our exploration, we delved into the molecular characteristics
and biological functions that distinguish these melanoma subtypes.
Utilizing the single-sample gene set enrichment analysis (ssGSEA)
algorithm, we quantified the enrichment of various molecular features
across the samples. Notably, we observed significant enrichment of
pathways such as epithelial-mesenchymal transition and hypoxia in the
CS2 subtype, which is associated with the worst prognosis. This finding
suggests that patients within the CS2 group might be less responsive to
immunotherapies (Fig. 3A). To probe into the transcriptional variances,
we scrutinized the potential regulatory elements and transcription factors
implicated in cancer chromatin remodeling. We discovered that
regulators like EGFR and TP63 were markedly activated in the CS2
subtype, whereas KAT5 and SIRT2 were specifically enriched in the CS3
subtype. These regulators are pivotal in the precise modulation of gene
expression, influencing cell fate and presenting themselves as potential
targets for cancer therapeutics. Our findings underscore the dynamic
interplay between gene regulation and epigenetic alterations in the
progression of SKCM (Fig. 3B). Acknowledging the pivotal role of tumor
immunity in tumorigenesis and disease progression, we employed the
CIBERSORT algorithm to quantify the infiltration levels of immune cells
across different molecular subtypes. We also examined the expression
of co-suppressive immune checkpoints and evaluated the immunity
score, stroma score, and methylation score of the samples. Our results

indicated a significant upregulation of immune checkpoint expression in
the CS1 subtype, hinting at the activation of pathways involved in
immune regulation within this group (Fig. 3C). Building on the genes
differentially expressed between subtypes, we identified and selected
genes that were uniquely upregulated in each subtype to serve as
classifiers. These were then validated in an external GEO cohort,
reinforcing the robustness of our subtype classification (Fig. 3D).
Furthermore, we assessed the concordance between the subtypes
identified, the NTP algorithm, and the PAM algorithm, providing a
comprehensive view of the subtypes’ consistency and reliability (Fig. 3E-
G).
Fig. 3
[image]
Molecular characterization of the consensus molecular subtypes for
SKCM synthesis. (A) Enrichment heatmap of cancer-associated
signature features of the three molecular subtypes. (B) Activity analysis
of 23 transcription factors and regulators associated with chromatin
remodeling. (C) Immunological characterization of different molecular
subtypes in the TCGA cohort. Heatmaps show differences in TME
scores, MeTIL, expression of immune checkpoint genes, and immune
cell infiltration. (D) Validation of molecular subtyping in the GEO cohort.
(E) Consistency of molecular subtyping with PAM in the TCGA-SKCM
cohort. (F) Consistency of molecular subtyping with NTP in the TCGA-
SKCM cohort. (G) Concordance of NTP with PAM in the GEO cohort
Full size image

Construction of machine learning-driven signature
(MLDS)
Using the “MOVICS” toolkit, we identified a total of 300 mRNAs that were
specifically upregulated in three cancer subtypes (CSs). These mRNAs
are closely related to the molecular classification of melanoma patients
and were used for further analysis in our study (Supplementary Table).
The TCGA-SKCM cohort, comprising 469 subjects, was designated as
our training dataset. Subsequently, the GSE19234, GSE59455, and
GSE65904 datasets were utilized as our validation cohorts for the
machine learning algorithms. In the initial phase, we employed univariate
Cox regression to select 33 prognostic genes from both the TCGA and
GEO datasets, with a stringent P-value threshold of 0.05. These genes
were then integrated as the foundation for our machine learning-driven
approach, culminating in the development of the machine learning-driven
signature (MLDS). A meticulous 10-fold cross-validation process was
conducted on 101 machine learning-integrated prognostic models within
the training dataset, with each model’s C-index determined across all
datasets. It is imperative to emphasize that a model’s efficacy is
evaluated based on its consistent performance across both the training
and validation datasets. Based on the highest C-index from the TCGA
training set, we selected the optimal model, a blend of Random Survival
Forest (RSF) and Partial Least Squares Regression with Cox Model
(plsRcox). With the highest average C-index (0.66) across the three
validation sets, it was chosen for its superior predictive accuracy
(Fig. 4A). This model was selected for its superior predictive accuracy.

Ultimately, a subset of 24 genes pivotal to molecular classification was
identified to construct the MLDS. We then calculated MLDS scores for
each participant across all cohorts. It was observed that individuals with
higher MLDS scores exhibited a trend towards poorer overall survival
(OS) in the TCGA-SKCM, GSE19234, GSE59455, and GSE65904
cohorts (Fig. 4B-E). Notably, the area under the ROC curve (AUC)
substantiated the high efficacy of our MLDS in predicting OS for
melanoma patients within the TCGA and GEO cohorts (Fig. 4F-I),
underscoring its potential clinical utility in prognostic assessment.
Fig. 4
[image]
Construction and validation of the machine learning-driven signature
(MLDS). (A) Multiple machine learning combinations were employed to
construct prognostic signatures related to molecular subtypes. The
values in the heatmap represent the C-index of the corresponding
models used for prognostic prediction; the bar chart on the right shows
the average C-index across multiple datasets. Ultimately, the best model
was constructed using the Random Survival Forest (RSF) combined with
Partial Least Squares Regression Cox (plsRcox) method. (B-E) Kaplan-
Meier survival curves of OS for patients in the high MLDS score group
and low MLDS score group in the GSE19234, GSE59455, GSE65904,
and TCGA cohorts. (F-I) ROC curves for the RSF + plsRcox model in the
GSE19234, GSE59455, GSE65904, and TCGA cohorts
Full size image

Nomogram construction and validation of clinical traits
In the TCGA cohort, based on the results of univariate Cox and
multivariate Cox analyses, MLDS could be an independent prognostic
indicator for patients compared to the clinical indicators of age, grade,
and stage (Fig. 5A, B). To extend the clinical usability of the constructed
machine learning-driven signature to help clinicians make better clinical
decisions, based on the correlation between the above clinicopathologic
features and risk scores, we created a nomogram for predicting the 1-,
3-, and 5-year survival rates of SKCM patients (Fig. 5C). From the model
results, age, stage, and MLDS had a greater impact on predicting OS,
indicating that a signature based on 24 genes related to typing could
better predict the prognosis of SKCM patients. The calibration curve can
also show that the nomogram can make an accurate prediction (Fig. 5D).
In addition to this, the area under the curve of the 3-year nomogram
score is much higher than that of other clinicopathologic signatures
(Fig. 5E). The 3-year DCA curve (Fig. 5F) and the C-index values
(Fig. 5G) both indicate that our constructed nomograms have the highest
net benefit and that the impact of the machine-learning-driven signatures
is more influential than the traditional models in clinical decision-making.
We show the associations of different MLDS subgroups and clinical
features, as well as the expression of all modeled genes, while MLDS is
mainly associated with the age of the patients (Fig. 5H, I).
Fig. 5
[image]
Independent prognostic analysis of MLDS score and clinicopathologic

factors in the TCGA cohort. (A, B) Univariate and multivariate Cox
regression analyses of clinicopathologic variables and MLDS scores in
the TCGA training cohort. (C) Nomograms of age, grade, and stage were
used to predict survival at 1, 3, and 5 years in SKCM patients. (D)
Correction curves for nomograms. (E) AUC values of MLDS score and
clinical characteristics at 3 years in the TCGA cohort. (F) DCA curves for
MLDS scores, Nomogram scores, and other clinical characteristics. (G)
Evaluation of the predictive effect of different clinical characteristics,
nomogram scores, and MLDS scores using c-exponential curves. (H)
Heatmap showing the expression of 24 model genes grouped by
different MLDS scores and the distribution of clinical features. (I) The
proportion of clinical stage in different MLDS score subgroups. *P < 0.05;
***P < 0.001
Full size image
MLDS is closely related to immune function
In our quest to understand the immune landscape as indicated by the
MLDS score, we delved into the correlation between the MLDS score
and the presence of immune cells and immune checkpoint expression.
Utilizing seven distinct methodologies, we computed the immune
infiltration scores within the TCGA dataset (Fig. 6A). To deepen our
comprehension of the link between MLDS scores and immune functions,
we employed the ssGSEA technique to quantify the various
subpopulations of immune cells. The ‘estimate’ tool was leveraged to
determine the proportions of stromal and immune cells across risk

groups, thereby providing an estimation of tumor purity. Given the
profound implications of the dysregulated expression and functionality of
immune checkpoint molecules (ICs) on the efficacy of tumor
immunotherapy, we scrutinized the expression profiles of these
checkpoints across different MLDS subgroups. Our findings indicated
that the subgroups with lower MLDS scores exhibited elevated immune
cell infiltration scores, and also displayed heightened expression of ICs
(Fig. 6B). Figure 6C illustrates the disparities in tumor mutational burden
(TMB) and the frequency of mutant genes between the groups with high
and low MLDS scores. Further correlation analysis revealed that the
majority of the genes integral to our model were positively correlated with
the genes encoding immune checkpoints. In contrast, the MLDS scores
exhibited a negative correlation with the expression levels of these
immune checkpoint molecules (Fig. 6D). This suggests a complex
interplay between the MLDS score, immune cell infiltration, and the
expression of immune checkpoints, which could have a significant
bearing on the responsiveness of melanoma patients to
immunotherapeutic interventions.
Fig. 6
[image]
Correlation between MLDS score and immunization in the TCGA cohort.
(A) Heatmap reflecting differences in immune infiltration scores between
high and low MLDS score groups. (B) Heatmap illustrating differences in
TME scores, immune checkpoint expression, and immune cell infiltration
in different MLDS score groups. (C) Comparison of mutation frequency

and tumor mutation load in different MLDS scoring groups. (D) Bubble
plot showing the correlation between common immune checkpoints and
MLDS model genes
Full size image
MLDS links to clinical care and cancer pathways
Immune checkpoint inhibitors are therapeutic agents that bolster the
immune system’s capacity to identify and combat cancer cells,
demonstrating remarkable potential in treating certain malignancies. To
further elucidate the influence of the MLDS on immunotherapy efficacy
and ICI responsiveness, we conducted a detailed analysis of the
relationship between MLDS scores and characteristics indicative of ICI
response. Our findings revealed a significant positive correlation
between the MLDS score and the proteasome, while a significant
negative correlation was observed with other immune checkpoint
blockade (ICB) response features. Moreover, the MLDS score exhibited
a substantial negative correlation with pivotal phases of the tumor
immune cycle, including the presentation of cancer antigens (step 2),
initiation and activation (step 3), T-cell recruitment (step 4), and the
destruction of cancer cells (step 7) (Fig. 7A). Individuals who would
benefit from immunotherapy can be identified using the Immune Profile
Score (IPS). Violin plots show the correlation between IPS values and
MLDS subgroups, with higher IPS values indicating enhanced responses
to PD-1 and CTLA-4 inhibitors. Whether PD-1 or CTLA-4 inhibitors were
used alone or in combination with these two immunotherapies, the low-

scoring subgroups showed a better response to immunotherapy
(Fig. 7B). The IC50 value, representing the drug concentration that
inhibits 50% of cell growth, is a critical parameter in pharmacology. We
discovered a significant positive correlation between the IC50 values of
cisplatin and temozolomide with MLDS scores, implying that patients
with lower MLDS scores might exhibit greater sensitivity to these
chemotherapeutic agents (Fig. 7C). Gene Set Enrichment Analysis
(GSEA) indicated an enrichment of the epithelial-mesenchymal transition
(EMT) pathway in the group with low MLDS scores (Fig. 7D). We further
investigated the prognostic significance of MLDS model genes across
various cancer types using univariate Cox analysis. Of particular interest,
AGPAT2 was the only risk gene identified for melanoma, and thus we
selected AGPAT2 for subsequent experimental analysis (Fig. 7E). Lastly,
we examined the connection between MLDS model genes and the
HALLMARK pathways. Oxidative phosphorylation, integral to
tumorigenesis, progression, metastasis, and immune evasion, showed a
positive association with AGPAT2 in many tumor types, with a
particularly notable link in melanoma (Fig. 7F). These findings
underscore the multifaceted role of MLDS in the complex interplay
between melanoma biology, treatment response, and immune
interactions.
Fig. 7
[image]
Correlation between MLDS score and clinical treatment in the TCGA
cohort. (A) Correlation of MLDS scores with ICB response characteristics

and immunoregulatory processes. (B) Comparison of IPS in MLDS high
and low SKCM groups of patients. (C) Correlation between MLDS score
and IC50 values for cisplatin/temozolomide. (D) Results of gene set
enrichment analysis of epithelial-mesenchymal transition in the MLDS
subgroup. (E) Heatmap of the prognostic value of model genes in the
TCGA pan-cancer cohort. (F) Gene set enrichment analysis results
about oxidative phosphorylation pathway for model genes in the TCGA
pan-cancer cohort
Full size image
AGPAT2 expression and signaling communication in
single-cell sequencing
In the initial phase, we conducted a dimensionality reduction and
clustering analysis on the single-cell sequencing data from three limbal
melanoma cases, sourced from the GSE215120 dataset. This process
involved categorizing the clusters based on the distinctive markers they
expressed, which facilitated the identification of various cell types,
including melanoma cells, T/NK cells, proliferating cells, endothelial cells,
fibroblasts, myeloid cells, and smooth muscle cells, as depicted in
Fig. 8A and B. Notably, the expression of AGPAT2 was predominantly
observed in melanoma and T/NK cells (Fig. 8C). Further analysis
segregated melanoma cells into two groups: those expressing AGPAT2
(AGPAT2+) and those not expressing it (AGPAT2-), as shown in Fig. 8D.
Utilizing the “monocle” R package, we delineated the developmental
trajectories and pseudotime distributions of epithelial cells within the

tumor tissues, revealing five distinct cell states during melanoma
progression. Intriguingly, AGPAT2 + melanoma cells were predominantly
found in the intermediate stages of cell development (Fig. 8E). The
“cellcall” R package, a powerful tool for deducing intercellular
communication networks and internal regulatory signals by
amalgamating both intracellular and extracellular signals, was employed
in our study. The circular graph in Fig. 8F illustrates the intensity of
ligand-receptor signaling among different cell types, with AGPAT2 + 
epithelial cells demonstrating enhanced communication with fibroblasts.
Additionally, our analysis of signaling pathway activities revealed a
robust MAPK signaling pathway interaction specifically between
AGPAT2 + epithelial cells and fibroblasts (Fig. 8G).
Fig. 8
[image]
Expression and role of AGPAT2 in single-cell sequencing. (A) Spatial
presentation of cell type annotation results. (B) Proportion of distribution
of different cell types in the six samples. (C) UMAP showing AGPAT2
expression in single-cell sequencing. (D) Distribution of AGPAT2
expression-positive and expression-negative melanoma cells shown by
UMAP. (E) Cell trajectory and pseudotemporal analysis of melanoma
cells. (F) Ligand-receptor intensities of different cell types shown by
circle plots. (G) Bubble plots showing analysis of signaling pathway
activity in different cell types
Full size image

The expression and function of AGPAT2 was verified by
cellular experiments
We evaluated the role of AGPAT2 in human melanoma by A375, and
A875 cell lines. Knockdown of AGPAT2 inhibited the clone-forming ability
of melanoma cells (Fig. 9A-C). CCK-8 assay experiments showed that
the proliferative ability of both A375 and A875 cell lines was significantly
decreased after the knockdown of AGPAT2 (Fig. 9D, E). Transwell assay
showed that compared with the control group, the knockdown of
AGPAT2 significantly reduced the A375 and A875 cell lines in the
number of migrating and invading cancer cells (Fig. 9F, G, H). In wound
healing assays, AGPAT2 knockdown significantly reduced the wound
healing rate in A375 and A875 cells (Fig. 9I, J, K). Overall the
proliferation, migration, and invasion ability of melanoma cell lines were
significantly inhibited after AGPAT2 knockdown.
Fig. 9
[image]
Exploring the role of the key gene AGPAT2. (A, B, C) Clone formation
assay. Colony forming ability of the A375 cell line and the A875 cell line
was significantly decreased after AGPAT2 knockdown. (D, E) CCK-8
assay to detect the proliferation of the A375 cell line and A875 cell line 5
days after incubation. (F, G, H) Transwell assay showing reduced cell
migration invasion ability after AGPAT2 knockdown. (I, J, K) Wound
healing assay showed a significant decrease in cell migration formation
ability after AGPAT2 knockdown. * p < 0.05, ** p < 0.01

Full size image
Silencing AGPAT2 inhibits subcutaneous melanoma
growth in vivo
We subcutaneously injected the A375 human melanoma cell line
transfected with either the control group or the Si-AGPAT2 group into
nude mice and dynamically observed the body weight and tumor growth
of the mice. The results showed that the tumor weight and volume of
mice in the Si-AGPAT2 group were significantly reduced compared with
the control group, while there was no significant difference in body
weight between the two groups (Fig. 10A-D). Immunofluorescence
analysis of mouse tissues showed that the expression of Ki-67-positive
cells in the tumor tissues of mice in the Si-AGPAT2 group was
significantly reduced, suggesting that Si-AGPAT2 treatment was
effective in inhibiting tumor proliferation (Fig. 10E, F).
Fig. 10
[image]
Knockdown of AGPAT2 inhibits subcutaneous tumor growth in vivo. (A)
Representative image of a mouse tumor showing a comparison of tumor
size between the control and Si groups. (B) Comparison of tumor weight
between control and Si group mice. (C) Change in body weight during
the experiment. (D) Tumour volume growth curve. (E) HMB45 and Ki-67
immunofluorescence images showing Ki67-positive cells in the tumors of
control and Si mice. (F) Comparison of cell proliferation index between
the two groups. ***p < 0.001

Discussion
The regulation of gene activity is a complex process involving numerous
genetic and epigenetic mechanisms such as DNA methylation, gene
mutations, and chemical modifications of histones. In-depth analysis of
multi-omics data from patients enables us to understand more
comprehensively the regulatory networks of specific diseases. However,
most current studies focus on a single histological level, and the choice
of histological clustering methods is often influenced by researcher
preference, as these methods are widely used, their respective
limitations are magnified [25, 26]. In this study, a comprehensive analysis
revealed that melanoma patients can be classified into three molecular
subtypes with significant prognostic differences. In particular, the second
subtype (CS2) showed the poorest overall survival, a phenomenon that
may be related to the significant activation of its internal epithelial-
mesenchymal transition and hypoxia signaling pathways, which play a
key role in the aggressive growth of melanomas and resistance to
treatment. In addition, the low expression of immune checkpoint
molecules in CS2 may be a potential reason for the poor response to
immunotherapy in this subtype of patients.
Machine learning algorithms, particularly those employed in the creation
of predictive models, are renowned for their ability to handle vast and
intricate datasets. One of their key strengths lies in their capacity to
identify non-linear connections and intricate data patterns, which might

be elusive to conventional statistical techniques. This capability allows
them to uncover subtle insights and relationships that could be
overlooked by more traditional approaches. As a result, they are
increasingly being utilized in various fields, including healthcare, finance,
and technology, to enhance decision-making and forecast outcomes
more accurately [16]. Through machine learning, we can extract deep
features from multi-omics data that may be closely related to melanoma
prognosis [27]. Feature selection is a crucial step when constructing
prognostic models. Correct feature selection not only improves the
predictive accuracy of the model but also reduces the complexity of the
model and avoids overfitting. To ensure the generalization ability of the
model, we adopt a cross-validation approach to evaluate the
performance of the model. This approach reduces the model’s
dependence on a specific dataset and improves the applicability of the
model across different patient populations [28]. In this study, we carefully
selected features associated with melanoma prognosis through
univariate COX regression analysis and integration of multi-omics data,
which were subsequently used to train machine learning models. Our
study shows that MLDS exhibits high C-index values in both the training
and validation cohorts, which indicates its high accuracy in predicting the
prognosis of melanoma patients. C-index value is an important measure
of the discriminative power of a predictive model, and the closer the
value is to 1, the better the predictive power of the model. Therefore, the
high C-index value of the MLDS provides strong evidence for its use as a
prognostic prediction tool. The MLDS not only performs well as a stand-
alone prognostic indicator but also provides a more accurate prognostic

prediction when used in conjunction with traditional clinical
characteristics, such as a patient’s age and clinical stage. This combined
approach compensates for the possible limitations of a single indicator
and provides more comprehensive information for clinical decision-
making through a multifactorial approach.
The correlation between MLDS scores and the expression levels of
immune checkpoint molecules further emphasizes the importance of the
tumor microenvironment (TME) in melanoma prognosis. Immune
checkpoints, such as PD-1/PD-L1, are key molecules that regulate the
immune response. Their expression levels can influence the activity of T
cells and the immune escape capabilities of tumors. The degree of
immune cell infiltration can reflect the immunogenicity of the tumor and
its potential responsiveness to immunotherapy. High immune cell
infiltration is usually associated with a better prognosis, as it may imply
that the immune system is actively combating the tumor [29]. In the
tumor microenvironment of melanoma and other cancers, infiltration of
immune cells such as T cells, B cells, and natural killer (NK) cells is
strongly associated with patient prognosis. The immune infiltration
observed in the low MLDS subgroup is therefore likely to represent an
immunosuppressive microenvironment [11, 30]. Thus, the close
relationship between MLDS and immune function provides a new
perspective on immunotherapy for melanoma. In particular, we found a
significant correlation between MLDS scores and the response profiles to
immune checkpoint inhibitors (ICIs). Patients with low MLDS scores are
likely to exhibit greater immune cell activation and expression of immune
checkpoint molecules, suggesting a better response to immunotherapy.

Furthermore, TCIA analysis revealed that patients with low MLDS scores
are more likely to benefit from combined immunotherapy blocking PD-1
and CTLA-4. These findings underscore the importance of considering
tumor molecular subtypes and associated immune characteristics when
developing personalized immunotherapy regimens. In this study, the
analysis of chemotherapy revealed a link between melanoma molecular
subtypes and sensitivity to chemotherapeutic drugs. Specifically, we
observed a positive correlation between MLDS scores and the IC50
values of certain chemotherapeutic agents, indicating that melanoma
patients with low MLDS scores may exhibit higher sensitivity to
chemotherapeutic drugs such as cisplatin and temozolomide.
Additionally, gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) found enrichment of
the epithelial-mesenchymal transition (EMT) pathway in patients with low
MLDS scores, which may be related to the resistance of tumor cells to
chemotherapeutic agents [31]. These results suggest that the MLDS
score is not only useful for predicting the prognosis of melanoma patients
but may also serve as a biomarker for evaluating patients’
responsiveness to specific chemotherapeutic agents, providing valuable
information for the development of personalized chemotherapy
regimens.
Our constructed MLDS and further bioinformatics research preliminarily
demonstrate the critical role of AGPAT2 in the molecular typing and
prognostic progression of melanoma, and single-cell sequencing
analysis reveals the unique biological role played by AGPAT2-
expressing melanoma cells in the TME. AGPAT2 is involved in the
synthesis of phosphatidylinositol (PA) and triacylglycerol (TAG), which

are important molecules that make up cell membranes and energy
storage [32]. Cancer cells avoid lipotoxicity triggered by excess free fatty
acids and provide cells with energy reserves by increasing the uptake
and synthesis of fatty acids and converting them to triglycerides for
storage in lipid droplets. Enhanced enzymatic activity of the TAG
synthesis and lipid droplet formation pathways in tumor cells is a
metabolic strategy to adapt to their rapid proliferative demands [33].
Numerous investigations have documented higher levels of AGPAT2 in
cancerous cells and human malignancies, correlating with heightened
proliferation and movement of these cells, as well as augmented tumor
expansion and aggressiveness, culminating in unfavorable outcomes.
Furthermore, the suppression of AGPAT2 has been observed to curtail
the growth of cancer cells and improve their response to chemotherapy
treatments [34,35,36]. A recent examination has unveiled that AGPAT2
is selectively and precisely activated by HIF-1 in cancerous cells,
bolstering their survival and resistance to chemotherapy under low-
oxygen environments [37]. The profound impact of AGPAT2 expression
on cancer cell proliferation suggests its potential as a therapeutic target,
leading to the investigation and development of AGPAT2 inhibitors.
Preliminary findings from numerous studies indicate that small-molecule
AGPAT2 inhibitors can induce apoptosis and successfully reduce cancer
cell proliferation, tumor xenograft growth, and resistance to conventional
chemotherapy [38, 39]. However, so far the mechanistic studies related
to AGAPT2 and melanoma remain a gap, and understanding the
expression pattern of AGPAT2 in different cell types helps us to identify
the metabolic properties and potential therapeutic targets of melanoma

cells.
Our preliminary research indicates that signal communication between
AGPAT2 + tumor cells and other cell types may impact the
microenvironment and cell-to-cell interactions in melanoma. The MAPK
signaling pathway is a cascade of protein kinases that transmit signals
within the cell, regulating various biological processes, including cell
proliferation, differentiation, survival, and death. Our observations
suggest that the expression of AGPAT2 may affect myeloid cells and
fibroblasts in the tumor microenvironment, and the interactions of these
cells with tumor cells may influence the activity of tumor cells through the
MAPK signaling pathway [40]. In addition, AGPAT2 is a key oncogene in
MLDS, positively correlated with the oxidative phosphorylation pathway,
especially in melanoma. Given the metabolic plasticity of melanoma
cells, simultaneously blocking oxidative phosphorylation and glycolysis
may be an effective therapeutic approach. For example, using mTORC1
inhibitors to block oxidative phosphorylation and MAPK pathway
inhibitors to block glycolysis can restore the sensitivity of melanoma
resistant to BRAF and other MAPK pathway component inhibitors [41].
Finally, through a series of in vivo and ex vivo experiments, we verified
the expression and function of AGPAT2 in melanoma. Knocking down
AGPAT2 inhibited the clonogenic ability of melanoma cells, significantly
reduced the proliferative capacity of the A375 and A875 cell lines, and
decreased the number of migrating and invasive cancer cells in these
cell lines. In vivo, silencing AGPAT2 suppressed the growth of
subcutaneous melanoma in nude mice, and the tumor weight and

volume were significantly reduced in the AGPAT2-silenced group
compared to the control group, while there was no significant difference
in body weight between the two groups of mice. These findings suggest
that AGPAT2 may play a significant promotional role in the development
of melanoma and could be a potential therapeutic target. By inhibiting the
activity of AGPAT2, the proliferation, migration, and invasion of
melanoma cells can be reduced, which may improve the therapeutic
outcomes for patients with melanoma.
In this study, we utilized a variety of machine learning algorithms,
including Random Survival Forest (RSF) and Partial Least Squares
Regression of Cox models (plsRcox), to determine the optimal model
combination through 10-fold cross-validation. Each algorithm has its
strengths and limitations; thus, selecting the appropriate algorithm and
fine-tuning it is crucial for enhancing model performance. Although the
MLDS demonstrated good predictive performance in this research, its
applicability and stability across a broader patient population require
further validation. Future studies should consider validating the predictive
efficacy of MLDS in patient populations of diverse ethnicities and
geographic regions, and continuously refine the model based on new
data to improve its generalizability and the scope of its clinical
applications [42, 43]. Given the diversity and complexity of biomarkers,
future research might explore the integration of MLDS with other
biomarkers, such as protein and metabolite markers, to further enhance
the precision and reliability of prognostic predictions. This multi-
biomarker strategy could potentially reveal more profound insights into
disease progression and treatment responses. It should be noted that

our focus is on the continuous prediction of survival probability or risk,
rather than simple binary classification, and thus its applicability in the
clinical setting is limited or even non-existent. In addition, we plan to
conduct analyses of positive predictive value (PPV) and negative
predictive value (NPV) at specific thresholds in our future work to further
explore the clinical utility of our model.
Data availability
Publicly available datasets were analyzed in this study. The raw data for
this study were obtained from TCGA (https://xenabrowser.net/), GEO
(http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/) and MSigDB (https://www.gsea-
msigdb.org/) databases. The original code for this study can be found
here: https://www.jianguoyun.com/p/DS5u2_wQzIvmChiD8M0FIAA.

## 10. Multimodal Machine Learning‐Based Marker Enables Early Detection and Prognosis Prediction for Hyperuricemia
**Authors:** Lin Zeng, Pengcheng Ma, Zeyang Li, Shengxing Liang, Chengkai Wu, C. Hong, Yan Li, Hao Cui, Ruining Li, Jiaren Wang, Jing-zhe He, Wen-yuan Li, Lushan Xiao, Li Liu
**Year:** 2024  
**Citations:** 21  
**Source:** Semantic Scholar  
**DOI:** 10.1002/advs.202404047  
**URL:** https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/1ba87e843fc792e36000b687701e4cdcc19a901d  

**Abstract:** Hyperuricemia (HUA) has emerged as the second most prevalent metabolic disorder characterized by prolonged and asymptomatic period, triggering gout and metabolism‐related outcomes. Early detection and prognosis prediction for HUA and gout are crucial for pre‐emptive interventions. Integrating genetic and clinical data from 421287 UK Biobank and 8900 Nanfang Hospital participants, a stacked multimodal machine learning model is developed and validated to synthesize its probabilities as an in‐silico quantitative marker for hyperuricemia (ISHUA). The model demonstrates satisfactory performance in detecting HUA, exhibiting area under the curves (AUCs) of 0.859, 0.836, and 0.779 within the train, internal, and external test sets, respectively. ISHUA is significantly associated with gout and metabolism‐related outcomes, effectively classifying individuals into low‐ and high‐risk groups for gout in the train (AUC, 0.815) and internal test (AUC, 0.814) sets. The high‐risk group shows increased susceptibility to metabolism‐related outcomes, and participants with intermediate or favorable lifestyle profiles have hazard ratios of 0.75 and 0.53 for gout compared with those with unfavorable lifestyles. Similar trends are observed for other metabolism‐related outcomes. The multimodal machine learning‐based ISHUA marker enables personalized risk stratification for gout and metabolism‐related outcomes, and it is unveiled that lifestyle changes can ameliorate these outcomes within high‐risk group, providing guidance for preventive interventions.

---

## 11. Lipid Nanoparticles for Drug Delivery
**Authors:** Letao Xu, Xing Wang, Yun Liu, Guangze Yang, Robert J. Falconer, Chun‐Xia Zhao
**Year:** 2021  
**Citations:** 474  
**Source:** OpenAlex  
**DOI:** 10.1002/anbr.202100109  
**URL:** https://openalex.org/W3216282807  

**Abstract:** Lipid nanoparticles have attracted significant interests in the last two decades, and have achieved tremendous clinical success since the first clinical approval of Doxil in 1995. At the same time, lipid nanoparticles have also demonstrated enormous potential in delivering nucleic acid drugs as evidenced by the approval of two RNA therapies and mRNA COVID‐19 vaccines. In this review, an overview on different classes of lipid nanoparticles, including liposomes, solid lipid nanoparticles, and nanostructured lipid carriers, is first provided, followed by the introduction of their preparation methods. Then the characterizations of lipid nanoparticles are briefly reviewed and their applications in encapsulating and delivering hydrophobic drugs, hydrophilic drugs, and RNAs are highlighted. Finally, various applications of lipid nanoparticles for overcoming different delivery challenges, including crossing the blood–brain barrier, targeted delivery, and various routes of administration, are summarized. Lipid nanoparticles as drug delivery systems offer many attractive benefits such as great biocompatibility, ease of preparation, feasibility of scale‐up, nontoxicity, and targeted delivery, while current challenges in drug delivery warrant future studies about structure–function correlations, large‐scale production, and targeted delivery to realize the full potential of lipid nanoparticles for wider clinical and pharmaceutical applications in future.

---

## 12. Antibody tests for identification of current and past infection with SARS-CoV-2
**Authors:** Jonathan J Deeks, Jacqueline Dinnes, Yemisi Takwoingi, Clare Davenport, René Spijker, Sian Taylor-Phillips, Ada Adriano, Sophie Beese, Janine Dretzke, Lavinia Ferrante di Ruffano, Isobel M Harris, Malcolm J Price, Sabine Dittrich, Devy Emperador, Lotty Hooft, Mariska MG Leeflang, Ann Van den Bruel, Cochrane COVID-19 Diagnostic Test Accuracy Group
**Year:** 2020  
**Citations:** 731  
**Source:** OpenAlex  
**DOI:** 10.1002/14651858.cd013652  
**URL:** https://openalex.org/W3038022415  

**Abstract:** The sensitivity of antibody tests is too low in the first week since symptom onset to have a primary role for the diagnosis of COVID-19, but they may still have a role complementing other testing in individuals presenting later, when RT-PCR tests are negative, or are not done. Antibody tests are likely to have a useful role for detecting previous SARS-CoV-2 infection if used 15 or more days after the onset of symptoms. However, the duration of antibody rises is currently unknown, and we found very little data beyond 35 days post-symptom onset. We are therefore uncertain about the utility of these tests for seroprevalence surveys for public health management purposes. Concerns about high risk of bias and applicability make it likely that the accuracy of tests when used in clinical care will be lower than reported in the included studies. Sensitivity has mainly been evaluated in hospitalised patients, so it is unclear whether the tests are able to detect lower antibody levels likely seen with milder and asymptomatic COVID-19 disease. The design, execution and reporting of studies of the accuracy of COVID-19 tests requires considerable improvement. Studies must report data on sensitivity disaggregated by time since onset of symptoms. COVID-19-positive cases who are RT-PCR-negative should be included as well as those confirmed RT-PCR, in accordance with the World Health Organization (WHO) and China National Health Commission of the People's Republic of China (CDC) case definitions. We were only able to obtain data from a small proportion of available tests, and action is needed to ensure that all results of test evaluations are available in the public domain to prevent selective reporting. This is a fast-moving field and we plan ongoing updates of this living systematic review.

---

## 13. Engineering nano-drug biointerface to overcome biological barriers toward precision drug delivery
**Authors:** Saquib Waheed, Zhibin Li, Fangyingnan Zhang, Anna Chiarini, Ubaldo Armato, Jun Wu
**Year:** 2022  
**Citations:** 198  
**Source:** OpenAlex  
**DOI:** 10.1186/s12951-022-01605-4  
**URL:** https://openalex.org/W4293747581  

### Full Text
Waheed et al. Journal of Nanobiotechnology          (2022) 20:395  
https://doi.org/10.1186/s12951-022-01605-4
REVIEW
Engineering nano‑drug biointerface 
to overcome biological barriers toward precision 
drug delivery
Saquib Waheed1,2, Zhibin Li1, Fangyingnan Zhang1, Anna Chiarini3, Ubaldo Armato1,3 and Jun Wu1,3* 
Abstract 
The rapid advancement of nanomedicine and nanoparticle (NP) materials presents novel solutions potentially capa-
ble of revolutionizing health care by improving efficacy, bioavailability, drug targeting, and safety. NPs are intriguing 
when considering medical applications because of their essential and unique qualities, including a significantly higher 
surface to mass ratio, quantum properties, and the potential to adsorb and transport drugs and other compounds. 
However, NPs must overcome or navigate several biological barriers of the human body to successfully deliver 
drugs at precise locations. Engineering the drug carrier biointerface can help overcome the main biological barriers 
and optimize the drug delivery in a more personalized manner. This review discusses the significant heterogene-
ous biological delivery barriers and how biointerface engineering can promote drug carriers to prevail over hurdles 
and navigate in a more personalized manner, thus ushering in the era of Precision Medicine. We also summarize the 
nanomedicines’ current advantages and disadvantages in drug administration, from natural/synthetic sources to clini-
cal applications. Additionally, we explore the innovative NP designs used in both non-personalized and customized 
applications as well as how they can attain a precise therapeutic strategy.
Keywords:  Nanoparticle, Biological barriers, Drug delivery, Nanomedicine

Introduction
Nanotechnology is a broad horizon of emerging tech-
nologies that can be used to enhance medical treatments. 
Nanoparticles (NPs) are solid particles or particulate dis-
persions that contain nanocapsules and nanospheres and 
are usually produced using polymerization techniques 
and preformed polymers [1]. Engineered nanoparticles 
(ENPs) have tremendous potential for increasing dis-
ease detection and therapy specificity. Nanomedicine 
is a multidisciplinary field that encompasses the fusion 
of biotechnology, nanotechnology, and information 
technology, resulting in many applications, including 
drug delivery and in vivo treatments, diagnostic devices, 
molecular imaging, biomarkers, regenerative medicine, 
and biosensors, etc. [2]. Nanotechnology offers multiple 
benefits to overcome the constraints associated with con-
ventional drug administration methods through site-spe-
cific and target-oriented conveyances. NPs have shown 
great potential to enhance the solubility and stability of 
encapsulated cargos, improve cell uptake, and lengthen 
circulation duration, which altogether helps increase 
safety and effectiveness [3, 4]. As a result of these fac-
tors, researches on NPs have been widely conducted, 
yielding encouraging findings in vitro and in vivo (small 
animal models) [5]. However, delivering a drug to its tar-
geted location is still particularly challenging regardless 
of these apparent merits.

Page 2 of 25
Waheed et al. Journal of Nanobiotechnology          (2022) 20:395 
Recent research showed that only 1% of the intrave-
nously delivered NPs reach their intended target sites 
[6]. Efforts have been made to increase drug carrier 
targeting efficiency by attaching cell receptor-targeted 
ligands to the particle surface [7]. Although this is a sig-
nificant step forward in achieving targeted drug deliv-
ery, the drug carrier must have to overcome various 
biological obstacles throughout the body to reach the 
specific target of interest. Even after arriving at their 
sites of action, prior to the release of their cargo and 
clearance, NPs must effectively penetrate through the 
cell membrane and navigate intracellular compartment 
networks to get to their subcellular mark. Apart from 
carrier biodistribution, various other impediments 
require proper overcoming. Despite the comprehensive 
research prompted by world-renown scientists around 
the globe, the number of nanomedicines approved by 
the Food and Drug Administration (FDA) is far less 
than anticipated, in part due to the translational gaps 
between animal model species and human trials out-
comes, which are not as satisfying as expected [5, 8]. 
These gaps stem from a persisting lack of knowledge of 
the physiological and pathological differences between 
animal models and humans, particularly concerning the 
mechanisms by which these variations shape nanomed-
icines’ distribution and effectiveness inside the body 
[9]. Clinical translation of therapeutics is hampered by 
several factors, of which species differences are not the 
least ones. Also, patient heterogeneity can hinder nano-
medicines’ efficacy as currently, only a limited number 
of clinical trials are available on nanomedicines interac-
tions with stratified patient groups. As a result, only a 
few nanomedicines are presently suggested as first-line 
alternative treatments, and many of them only benefit 
a small proportion of patients [10]. This underappreci-
ated variability in the biological foundations of diseases 
and across patients partially affects NPs effectiveness 
by altering the development, shape, and physiology of 
the pathology-affected tissue.
Considering the vast number of publications and the 
speed at which data is updated, this study explores the 
progress and applications of Nanotechnology in the 
realm of Nanomedicine through a broad, comprehen-
sive overview of the most commonly investigated NPs. 
Because we expect that Precision Medicine therapies 
will profoundly affect precision NPs in the near future, 
we discuss critical biological barriers to targeted thera-
pies involving precision drug delivery and their clini-
cal transition to improve patient-specific therapeutic 
responses. Additionally, we discuss the challenges 
and opportunities associated with the most promis-
ing and demanding applications of NPs in modern 
Nanomedicine.
Classes of NPs
Lipid‑based NPs
The past decade has witnessed significant attention from 
scientists toward the Lipid-based NPs (LBNPs), which, 
due to their biocompatibility, were even considered 
“nanosafe” carriers. LBNPs present various shapes and 
sizes, but the most common are vesicle-like spherical 
platforms made by at least one lipid bilayer surround-
ing an aqueous interior compartment (Fig.  1). LBNPs 
can transport large payloads and exhibit various phys-
icochemical properties that can be manipulated to alter 
their biological features [11]. Such vesicles can upload 
hydrophobic and hydrophilic pharmaceuticals [12, 13], 
including vaccines [14], insulin [15], siRNAs [16], pro-
teins [17], and enzymes [18]. They can also be admin-
istered intravenously, orally, or transdermally to treat 
disorders including Alzheimer’s disease, cancer, and dia-
betes [19, 20]. For these reasons, LBNPs are common to 
most FDA-approved nanomedicines [10, 21].
Since liposomes were first characterized by Alec Bang-
ham in 1960, liposomes sparked attention for several 
decades, most notably when Doxil® was licensed by the 
FDA and utilized in clinical trials [20]. Liposomes are the 
largest category of LBNPs, primarily made of phospho-
lipids, and can take on unilamellar or multilamellar vesic-
ular shapes [22]. Liposomes are remarkable drug delivery 
vehicles as their composition resembles cell membranes. 
Additionally, they are also biocompatible and biodegrad-
able and can improve drug stability and biodistribution. 
Generally, NPs stability in  vivo and in  vitro is mainly 
affected by their shapes, sizes, surface charges, surface 
modifications, and preparation methods; these char-
acteristics can be modified during their synthesis [23]. 
Liposomes are frequently modified (with polymers or 
ligands) to improve circulation and distribution, making 
them even more suitable for clinical usage [24, 25]. Sev-
eral liposome-based biomedical applications are either 
in clinical trials or soon on the market [26–28]. A recent 
study examined the ability of Pluronic F127 (PF127)- and 
PEGylated liposomes to penetrate a pathological mucus 
obtained from chronic obstructive pulmonary disease 
(COPD). The PEG-liposomes penetrated the mucus bar-
rier effectively after 27  h of incubation [29]. Similarly, 
nanoscale diagnostic agents can be enclosed within 
theranostic liposomes for imaging purposes, wherein 
the therapeutic agent can be encapsulated in the core or 
incorporated in the lipophilic bilayer shell [30–32]. For 
example, to improve magnetic resonance imaging (MRI), 
superparamagnetic iron oxides (SPIONs) can be coated 
with a lipid layer or encapsulation of gadolinium(III) che-
lates in the aqueous core of liposomes. Likewise, Li et al. 
demonstrated the multifunctional liposome containing 
lipidized gadolinium-DOTA lipids not only could be used 

Page 3 of 25
Waheed et al. Journal of Nanobiotechnology          (2022) 20:395 
	
as an MRI imaging probe but also suitable for near-infra-
red fluorescence imaging relying on the lipidized near-
infrared dyes [33]. Additionally, LBNPs are currently 
being highlighted as an instrumental component of the 
COVID-19 mRNA vaccines, in which they play a major 
role in protecting and transporting mRNA into the cells 
[14, 34]. With the successful use of LNPs as a delivery 
vector for the COVID-19 mRNA vaccines, the research 
horizons for mRNA vaccines will likely be broadened.
Lipid nanoparticles (LNPs) are another prominent 
subgroup of LBNPs and often employed for nucleic acid 
delivery. They differ from standard liposomes because of 
their particulate core, and they produce micellar struc-
tures that can be changed depending on the preparation 
methods and formulation parameters [35]. LNPs are usu-
ally made of four key components: phospholipids assist-
ing in particle structure; cholesterol aiding membrane 
fusion and stability; cationic or ionic lipids combining 
with negatively charged genetic material to promote 
endosomal escape; and PEGylated lipids improving 
circulatory conveyance and stability [36]. LNPs have 
increased their relevance to applications involving cus-
tomized genetic treatments due to their efficacy in 
nucleic acid delivery, as well as to their small size, ease 
of synthesis, and stability in the serum [17]. In order to 
administer these nucleic acid-based therapeutics, ioniz-
able LNPs are a robust delivery platform because they 
have a physiological near-neutral pH. However, once 
endocytosed, they become fully charged in the acidic 
endosomal compartments, facilitating their endosomal 
escape for intracellular drug delivery [37, 38]. Despite 
these benefits, LNP systems might be hampered by poor 
drug loading and biodistribution, resulting in their signif-
icant uptake by the liver and spleen [21].
Polymeric NPs
Polymeric nanoparticles (PNPs) are colloidal systems 
synthesized from either natural or synthetic polymers 
and classified into nanocapsules and nanospheres. PNPs 
have been extensively explored in Nanomedicine due to 
their surface modification capabilities, they may be syn-
thesized to finely control NPs characteristics. For exam-
ple, drugs can be loaded inside the PNP’s core cavity or 
chemically bonded to the NP’s surface. PNPs are also 
excellent vehicles appropriate for co-delivery applications 
because they can carry both hydrophobic and hydrophilic 
drugs and a wide range of cargos with different molecu-
lar weights [39–41]. To date, PNP-based materials have 
been increasingly employed in nanocomposites, cancer 
medication delivery, and photovoltaic applications. The 
smaller particle size of PNPs is one of the key aspects 
that makes them different from their counterparts. PNPs 
could be further enhanced and precisely controlled due 
to their specific qualities to attain desirable attributes for 
improved biocompatibility and bioavailability [42, 43].
Nanospheres are slightly bigger colloidal NPs 
(indeed, their shape is not invariably spherical); nano-
capsules, on the other hand, resemble a vesicular sys-
tem composed of a polymeric shell. Within these two 
broad groups, NPs are further classified according to 
Fig. 1  The diverse types of nanoparticles. Nanoparticles (NPs) could be classified into four main categories according to size, shape, and 
physicochemical properties. Each class of NPs includes several subclasses, some of which are highlighted here. Each class has numerous advantages 
and disadvantages regarding conveyed cargo, delivery, and patient responses

Page 4 of 25
Waheed et al. Journal of Nanobiotechnology          (2022) 20:395 
their morphology into polymersomes, micelles, and 
dendrimers. For instance, polymersomes are artificial 
vesicles that have copolymer membranes endowed with 
amphiphilic blocks. They are similar to liposomes in 
that they typically are locally responsive. Still, they have 
better stability and a higher cargo retention efficiency 
[44], making them serve as valuable vehicles for deliv-
ering drugs to the cytosol [45].
Poly(dimethylsiloxane) (PDMS) and poly(ethylene gly-
col) (PEG) are two smart polymers that are often copoly-
merized as polymeric micelles for medical applications. 
Polymeric micelles are nanoscale drug delivery systems. 
They are responsive block copolymers self-assembled 
into nanospheres with a hydrophobic core and a hydro-
philic coating that protects an aqueous drug cargo and 
promotes circulation. Polymeric micelles are generally 
flexible carriers of a variety of payloads and therapeutics 
and have been utilized to deliver cancer therapies in clini-
cal studies [46]. In a doxorubicin (DOX)-resistant ovarian 
cancer cell spheroid model, PEG-phosphatidylethanola-
mine (PEG2000-PE) micelles loaded with DOX and tar-
geting with anti-nucleosome mAb 2C5 were found to 
be effective. Compared to free DOX and non-targeted 
DOX micelles, the 2C5 targeted DOX micelles showed 
improved uptake and penetration abilities and finally 
induced greater cell death [47]. Similarly, in another case, 
Hu et al. proposed a nanoplatform with paclitaxel (PTX) 
encapsulated within a triblock Polycaprolactone (PCL)-
PEG-PCL copolymer that confirmed sustained drug 
release in combination with circadian chrono modu-
lated chemotherapy [48]. Moreover, Hong et al. obtained 
image-guided polymeric micelles containing DOX and 
SPIONs NPs in a folate-conjugated PEG-b-PCL copoly-
mer [49]. These studies offer superior treatment effi-
cacy and real-time tracking of the drug delivery system 
in  vivo, which is crucial for designing more promising 
drug delivery systems.
Dendrimers are highly branched polymers with irreg-
ular or complex 3D structures. Due to their highly con-
trolled size, shape, mass, and surface modifications can 
incorporate various therapeutics to generate physiologi-
cally active drug conjugates. On the outside, dendrimers 
own active functional groups that allow external con-
trast agents or biomolecules to be conjugated while the 
therapeutics can be loaded inside the shell. Dendrimers 
can carry a variety of payloads, although they are most 
typically used to deliver nucleic acids and other smaller 
compounds [50]. The use of charged Polyamidoamine 
(PAMAM) dendrimers is common as they are a prime 
example of these versatile applications. Numerous com-
pounds derived from dendrimers are now being evalu-
ated in clinical trials, including transfection agents, 
contrast agents, and topical gels [50, 51].
In general, PNPs are excellent candidates for drug 
delivery due to their biodegradability, water-solubility, 
and biomimetic properties. Their surface can be read-
ily modified to facilitate further targeting [30], enabling 
them to transport genetic material, therapeutics, and 
proteins to specific organs or tissues. Therefore, PNPs are 
valuable tools in gene therapy, diagnostics, and the suc-
cessful treatment of various diseases. Regardless of the 
benefits, PNPs entail a higher risk of toxicity and parti-
cle aggregation. Although a limited number of polymeric 
nanomedicines have been authorized by the FDA and are 
already being used in clinics, many new generations of 
polymeric nanocarriers have already been preclinically 
tested and are currently evaluated in clinical trials [10].
Inorganic NPs
Recent breakthroughs in Nanotechnology have permitted 
the use of several inorganic compounds, including iron, 
gold, and silica, to synthesize NPs for diverse drug deliv-
ery applications (Fig. 1). Inorganic NPs can be designed 
and synthesized to achieve a broad range of shapes, 
sizes, and geometries. The most thoroughly studied gold 
NPs (AuNPs) are fabricated in various shapes, includ-
ing nanoshells, nanospheres, nanocages, and nanorods 
[52]. Furthermore, due to the qualities of the constituent 
materials, inorganic NPs show distinct optical, electronic, 
magnetic, and thermo-physical or mechanical properties. 
For instance, the photothermal characteristics of AuNPs 
are attributed to the presence of delocalized free elec-
trons that continuously oscillate at a frequency depend-
ing on the particle’s size, shape, and structure [53]. 
Additionally, functionalization can easily modify AuNPs 
to enhance their characteristics and ability to transport 
drugs, thus making them ideal for biomedical and bio-
logical applications [52].
Iron oxide NPs (IONPs), which hold a privileged posi-
tion among the bulk of FDA-approved potential inor-
ganic nanomedicines, is another extensively studied 
material for inorganic NP synthesis [54]. Magnetic IONPs 
are composed of maghemite (γ-Fe2O3) and magnetite 
­(Fe3O4) and show superparamagnetic characteristics at 
particular sizes (diameters ranging from 1 and 100 nm). 
They have demonstrated effectiveness as thermal-based 
therapeutics, contrast agents, and drug-delivery systems 
[55]. Many researchers have used magnetic hyperthermia 
in combination with traditional methods to treat vari-
ous types of cancers following the first clinical applica-
tion of magnetic hyperthermia in 2011 [56, 57]. In recent 
research, SPIONs have been packaged into exosomes 
derived from mesenchymal stem cells. These exosomes 
are efficiently internalized by tumor cells, supporting 
remote targeting and ablation of tumor cells. Meanwhile, 
SPIONs coated with lipids can also be a carrier for poorly 

Page 5 of 25
